OFFICERS
JACKSONVILLE JEWISH FEDERATION
8505 San Jose Blvd. • Jacksonville, FL 32217 • (904) 448-5000
The Jacksonville Jewish Federation is the central fundraising, planning,
budgeting and coordinating agency for the Jewish community. It strives to ad¬
dress the needs of the Jewish community, encouraging community participa¬
tion. Its diverse committees offer many opportunities for volunteer involve¬
ment.
The Federation funds and coordinates community relations and education
programs. It publishes a monthly newspaper of Jewish news of local and na¬
tional interest. The Federation’s Israel and New York Teen Experiences and
Young Leadership program examine Jewish life and history and give partici¬
pants an opportunity to experience their Jewishness in unique and meaning¬
ful ways.
The Federation conducts an annual campaign that raises funds for the
United Jewish Appeal, as well as other national and overseas agencies. Local
beneficiaries are the Jewish Community Alliance, Jewish Family and Commu¬
nity Services, the Kosher Nutrition Program, River Garden Heorew Home for
the Aged and the chapters of various Jewish organizations.
President
Elliot Zisser
First Vice President
Steve Silverman
Second Vice President
Matthew Edelman
Treasurer
Joan Levin
Immediate Past President
Aaron M. Scharf
Executive Vice President
Isaac Lakritz
Assistant Director
Gail Meltzer
Jewish Family & Community Services
Iris Young, Executive Director
1988 Officers
President Lafer, Dr. Dennis
1st Vice President Harrison, Alan
Vice-Presidents Epstein, M. David
Merritt, Dr. Stephen
Winterfield, Cathy
Wolf, Martin
Financial Vice-President Harrison, Alan
Medical Director Benrubi, Dr. Guy
Mental Health Director Mann, Dr. Milton
Treasurer Moss, Norman
We are a “Full-Service” Agency helping our population from the very young to the very old to cope
with social, psychological and environmental problems. We work to promote stability and continuity
of family life, security and development of the self for individuals and a finer quality of life for those who
come to us in a world in which discontinuity, crises, social disorder and insecurity sometimes seems to
rule.
It is time again to look backwards so that we can better understand how to move forward. In this
past year, we have the opportunity to test ourselves to see how well we are meeting the ongoing
challenges that we face, how to maintain and enrich our present services, while being open and
responsive to emerging issues and needs in our community.
There are many issues which we face. They include the “greying” of our population accompanied by cutbacks in public resources and consequent demands
upon the private sector.; the loosening of the ties of the physical community; more women employed outside of the home; and a redefinition of family structure,
relationships and male/female roles.
It is, therefore, not surprising that so many of our clients come to us for help in dealing with losses, anxieties, guilts,, fears and angers that interfere with
their family, social and business relationships and their own comfort with themselves.
During the past year, over eight thousand (8,000) units of service have been provided in the counseling area. Over three thousand (3,000) units of service
have been provided in the area of group work. Almost five thousand (5,000) unite of service have been provided in the area of financial assistance and we have
fed over eleven hundred (1,100) people from our food pantry this year. Nine thousand (9,000) unite of service have been provided to our senior adult population.
We have also provided Home Studies and information packets for adoptions.
Rather than provide charity, the charge of modem Jewish social work is to do tzedakah. It is an act of charity to provide food to the hungry. It is an act
of tzedakah to teach the hungry how to provide food for themselves. When social workers assess a person, they take into consideration the impact of the entire
family. Jewish practitioners are likely to be sensitive not only to the client’s immediate family, but to the larger Jewish community family and the impact it has
had on the upon the client.
Our agency is Jewish not because we speak the language, not because of the mezuzzah on the door or the Judaic art on the walls, but because of who we
are and who we have been. We are but one more example of what the Jewish people have to offer the community and what the community has to offer the
Jewish people.
There is no better way to share with you what we mean to the community than by letting you hear from those whose lives we’ve touched.
“You’ve help me understand a lot of things that I didn’t realize before.”
“ . . . has been just wonderful to me giving ... at a time when my need of a friend was urgent. I turn to your organization and . .. has been there for me
every step of the way.”
“Thank you for being so helpful at a very stressful time for me.”
“To the ’light at the end of the tunnel’ it’s great to have you as a friend. I’m not sure how I would last if I did not have you just sit and listen during rough
times. You have always been there to lend an ear or shoulder, and for that I am forever grateful. Your emotional support is very important to me and at times
if I did not have that I would go crazy.”
“My son was in severe emotional pain . . . helped open doors to ease his problems. When your child hurts and you don’t know how to help, I can only
say how indebted I am . . .”
“I couldn’t help but to turn out to be a winner when I had you and Jewish Family Services encouraging me and giving me good and substantial material to
fully be prepared.”
“From you personally, I have learned who I am and what I need to do for a successful professional and personal life. The structures that... have provided
me with will allow me to constantly re-evaluate my actions, thoughts and needs for my future.”
“Everyone makes you feel good about yourself.”
Our achievements are the result of deep commitments by staff and Board and our resources of financial support, primarily the Jacksonville Jewish Federation.
Our standards for performance are very high. We are always interested in evaluating what we do and learning how to do it better.
Together we look forward to dealing with the major challenge in our future; how to maintain a diversity of services and a high quality in those services in
the face of increased competition for available dollars and for highly qualified professionals.
This will be a year of many changes for our Agency. The Board of Directors has participated in a retreat specifically designed to do long range planning.
They will be looking at unmet needs in our community and new program offerings.
With the community’s help, many dreams can be turned into realities; for example, our biggest dream, a group home for the developmental^ disabled;
Adoption Family Network; “SUBSTANCE ABUSE: ISSUES AND ANSWERS” program; and a senior transportation service project, a much needed service
to help maintain our elderly population.
We’d like to hear from you as we move forward. Help us plan to help you.
Best wishes for a Happy, Healthy New Year.

Cantor Emeritus
Abraham Marion
Center Choral Society
On March 6, Cantor Emeritus Abraham Marton presented his annual Choral Society Concert
featuring guest soloists: Cantor David Lefkowitz of Park Avenue Synagogue in Manhattan, Cantor
Jerome B. Kopmar of Beth Abraham in Dayton, Ohio, and operatic soprano Irene Lumpkin of
Jacksonville. The choir was conducted by Daniel Marmorstein.
Our thanks to Cantor Marton for coordinating these very beautiful programs for our congregation
and community to enjoy.
We are grateful to the following Choral Society members: Celia Bressler, Gertrude Brown,
Raymond Cohen, Pollie Copans, Cynthia Disher, Margaret Fleet, Lois Gosa, Ira Grann, Dr.
Randall Haas, Iris Hill, Ruth Jacobsen, Rosalind Klein, Henry Lasris, Clara Mann, Marilyn
Mass, Charles Mizrahi, Leo Moskovitz, Peggy Organes, Natalie Osofsky, llene Reiter, Abba
Safer, Anita and Steve Solomon, Barbara Spiwak, Cathy Wilensky and the conductor, Judy
Geller.

===== PAGE 9 =====
Solomon Schechter Day School
A Day in the Life of a Day School Principal
“Mr. Kennedy, Josh pushed me into the lockers for no reason!”
“Mr. Kennedy, Mr is ill today. We need a substitute.”
“Willard, can we please get the students to the Social Hall with less noise?”
Phone message. ”Mrs called and is upset. Her son deserved an A in history.”
“Schedule change?!? What do you mean a schedule change?!?”
MEMO: “Don’t forget the Education Committee meeting tonight.”
“Mr. Kennedy, did you remember to put in the order for the new textbook?”
“Willard, the word on the street is !!”
It is now 9:30 A.M. and the day has begun in typical manner — normal for most day school principals
accustomed to leading a rather “fragmented” existence and always expecting the unexpected.
However, Solomon Schechter is not just a day school, it is a Jewish school, and because of that, I have learned each day to appreciate
that there is a distinctive tone or atmosphere that I have not experienced elsewhere. For this is a school that does indeed aim to educate the
total child, including the spiritual/moral dimension. This is a school which demonstrates what a school should be—a commitment to excellence
in all aspects of the school.
When I leave the present building, I observe the flurry of activity associated with the buildong expansion to better meet our needs; when
I observe incredible cooperation, dedication,, and hard work of so many parents, teachers, and staff; when I recall how supposedly immature
students take on the responsibility of conducting Student Council meetings, organize activities supporting the Feed A Needy Neighbor Program,
celebrate the coming of Shabbat at our Shabbat Dinners (and the list literally could go on and on), I have no doubt as to the purpose of the
Solomon Schechter Day School.
Hectic? Harried? Problems? Struggles? Of course. Is it worth it? You bet it is! For I am truly gratified to be able to share in the Center’s
commitment to education and its future.
We invite each of you to share in this exciting venture with us by becoming part of our Day School Program. Truly, together we can meet
our goals of “Knowledge shared, Heritage preserved.”

JACKSONVILLE JEWISH CENTER MASTER CALENDAR 1988-89
===== PAGE 14 =====
55+
One of the newest fun-loving groups has been formed at the Jacksonville
Jewish Center for Jewish men and women 55 years and older. The group meets
one Sunday afternoon a month for an entertaining program and refreshments.
An exciting year has already been planned. On October 9, back by popular
request, is bingo. On November 13, Mary Wolf will review, You Can't Fight City
Hall. There’s more in store for you. So come join us.
A happy and healthy New Year to all of you.
Officers
Program Co-Chairs
Helen Rosen & Grace Goldberg
Financial Secretary
Jack Silverman
Recording Secretary
Sada Freedman

Henry Kramer Library
& Resource Center
Chairman: Minnie Schreiber
Co-Chairman: Diane Fages and May Goldie Stillman
Belle Gendzier
Marsha Pollock
*Linda Bressler
Library Cart: Lillian Meizlik
Use Gottlieb
*Jay Aronson
Rita Gendzier Hermine Newman
Judaica Collection Florence Schreiber
Treasurer: Roslyn Selber
*Deceased
This has been the year for the Solomon Schecter Day School Library. Mrs. Pam Picray, School Librarian presented a
dynamic school library program full of displays and meaningful exhibits designed to encourage children to read so many
new books. These were made available by the “Book-A-Bration” program of the Solomon Schechter PTA headed by
capable Mrs. Edith Horovitz.
The Library Sisterhood Committees presented three Oneg Shabbat Book Reviews - “Memoirs of a Fortunate Jew” by Mrs.
Debbie Dreher, “Who Wrote the Bible” by Mrs. Joan Levin and “As a Driven Leaf” by Ms. Minnie Schreiber.
The Reading Circle is alive and well. The group meets on Sunday evenings every six weeks at member’s homes. All
Center members are welcome and invited to join in this pleasurable activity. Some of the books read this year are
“Terrorism - How the West Can Win” by Wm. B. Natanyahu, “Memoirs of a Fortunate Jew” by Dan V. Segre, “The
Strange Nation of Rafael Mendes” by Moacyr Scliar.
The Library Cart is available at the Library - Sisterhood Oneg Shabbaton. The Library is open on Sunday morning 9 AM
to 12 noon.
Many service hours are contributed by Library Committee members. We were greatly saddened by the loss of Linda
Bressler and of Jay Aronson, two active members whose assistance was greatly appreciated and will be missed.
The generous support of the Center and Center membership make possible purchase of new books and periodicals for
all members to enjoy.

Jacksonville Hevrah Kaddisha
OFFICERS
President - Joel Bressler
V. Pres. - Nathan Sandler
Treasurer - Alan Taffet
Telephone Chairman - Ann & Nathan Shmunes
OTHER MEMBERS ARE
Bea Browdy, Laurie Benwick, Babs Goldstein, Dr. Michael
Greenburg, David Mizrahi, Max Shumunes, Simon Selber,
Anita Solomon, Lil Meizlik, Lisa Lefkowitz, Bob Bossen,
Marlene Bossen and Janet Husney.
Dignity After Death
While the world around us continues to debate death with dignity and “when to pull the plug” on patients kept alive by artificial
means, few people appear to care what happens to the body after death has occurred.
For almost two thousand years, traditional Judaism has cared to treat the deceased with as much dignity as one would have in
life. Plain white shrouds to cover the dead replaced ostentatious garments. Public viewing of the remains was not allowed
—better to remember the deceased as they were in life.
Burial societies were formed in every town and city to make sure that standards of loving kindness were maintained for all.
Much respect was given to a member of the Holy Society, or Hevra Kaddisha, as these groups were called.
Jacksonville has been fortunate to have an active Hevra Kaddisha for almost thirteen years, although the number of active
members has been smaller than desired. Additional members can serve to enhance dignity after death even more than has been
done in the past.
A “Hevra” member serves without pay to handle some aspect of caring for the deceased or the remaining close relatives.
Various ways they can serve are:
(1) participating with other members of the committee to wash, shroud and place the body in a plain wooden coffin,
(2) telephoning other members of the committee to arrange for them to meet as the need arises,
(3) providing transportation for the Shomer, the person (or persons) who sits with the deceased from the time the
body has been placed in the casket until the funeral,
(4) preparing the Meal of Consolation, the first meal which the family members eat after they have returned from
the burial,
(5) making arrangements in the house of mourning for the 7 day Shiva period, including preparation of meals,
(6) baby-sitting the small children of the family when necessary, and so on.
“How can I expect someone to care for me after I die if 1 don’t help others who need me now?”
Please contact your rabbi or Joel Bressler, the local Hevra Kaddisha president, if you are interested in serving on our
committee of dedicated workers.
Alan Taffet, Nathan Sandler, Simon Selber;
Seated: Joel Bressler, Bea Browdy

Jewish War Veterans
Lt. Meyer Leibovitz Post 199, Jacksonville, Florida
After a busy year, Post 199 is still one of the major Posts in
the North-Central Florida District Council of J.W.V., as well
as being very active in the North-East Florida Veterans Coun¬
cil.
Once again, we have enjoyable speakers and programs at
our monthly meetings. At our July meeting we had Ms. Ann
Hyman, Features Columnist of the Florida-Times Union,
recounting her experiences and interesting visits to various
Russian historic cities.
We are very active and supportive of hospitalized Veterans in
Lake City and Gainesville Medical Centers, and are also
interested in Jacksonville Community affairs.
We had the largest turn-out of veteran organizations at the
Memorial Day services at Evergreen Cemetery.
Post 199 also led the parade marching with our colors and
the Israeli flag to the J.C.A. grounds with a large contingent
in honor of Israel’s 40th Anniversary. We also had an
information booth and even wrote new applicants.
Come join us in promoting Judaism and the community,
fight anti-semitism and help the Veterans!
Top row from left: Alexander Phillips, Kenneth Jacobson. 2nd
row from left: John Perin, Jakob Rogozinski, Marcus Grebler,
Murray Solomon. Kneeling: Hugh Schulman.

Groundbreaking Ceremony
It was a warm day with a light breeze. Just perfect for an outdoor event. Nature was with us. More than 400 people attended. Their
spirit was with us. Success was in the air because our members responded to the needs of our synagogue. The dream will now become a
reality.
Sisterhood President Marilyn Garber lights the Hanukkah Menorah
with the aid of Rabbi Dov Kentof.
Leonard Setzer, co-chairman of the campaign, thanks the campaign
workers and the congregation for their encouragement and support.
Max Horovitz fell asleep while listening
to Grandpa Shel.
Center leaders participating in symbolic groundbreaking: (l-r) Sol Proctor, Leonard Setzer,
Jack Selvan, Philip Selber, Jack Mizrahi, Gene Kornblum, Joe Honigman, Rabbi David Gaffney
. Center Sisterhood has always participated n the Center's Building

Blanche Slott Retires
It is with mixed emotions that we celebrated the retirement of
BLANCHE SLOTT. She has been a devoted employee for over forty
years, beginning as a volunteer and climbing to the position of Admini¬
strative Director. As Catering Director, Blanche has made arrangements
for life cycle celebrations for many congregants. She has been a friend
and family to us all. She distinguished herself as Sisterhood President
for three terms; organized three Girl Scout.Troops and five Brownie
troops; and served on the Center Board of Directors as Chairman of
the Youth Commission. Blanche has touched all of our lives.
We will miss Blanche very much, but are happy for her and the new
lifestyle she will enjoy in the years to come* We look forward to seeing
her at Center functions as a guest. We wish her the very best that life
has to offer, for she has given us the very best she had to offer.
Honoring Blanche were: Bob Gast, past executive director,
Lois Peltz-co-chair of the day, nephew, Ron Levin, Blanche,
Marsha Pollock, co-chair, and former executive director,
Bob Fischer.

Torah Readers
I Had a Dream July 17, 1988
The dream came after pondering various possible solutions to the Middle East
crisis. Iran is a country that has 21 of the 22 Arab nations against it (all except
Syria). Israel is a country that has 21 of the 22 Arab nations against it—all except
Egypt. Iran, after Khomeini, desperately wants to end the war against Iraq. Only
pride stands in the way. Iran therefore calls on Israel to join Iran in threatening Iraq
to admit that Iraq startd the war. Iraq concedes and the eight-year-old war ends. In
thankfulness, the 21 Arab nations recognize Israel’s right to existence and security
and the 40-year-old war ends.
Ted Gross
I had another dream. (Remember the Biblical story’s descriptions of Joseph’s second dream?). This
one was the result of considering ways to increase the number of Torah reading participants each
Shabbat morning. This dream suggested that we ask each Bar Mitzvah to spend one summer at home in
order to read, at least, one portion a week for 12 consecutive weeks. Like Joseph, I hope the dreams will
bear fruition.
The Talmud in Berachot 16b lists a number of personal prayers. That of Rabbi Eleazar is particularly
appropriate: “May it be Thy will to cause to dwell in our lot love and brotherhood and peace and
friendship, and mayest Thou make our borders rich in students and prosper our latter end with good
prospect and hope.”
We thank all the readers and the many Bar Mitzvah candidates who gave so unstintingly of their time
and effort to join our team this year. We especially thank the readers who took total responsibility for all
the readings of their sidrot—Ari and David Silverman and Jonathan and Douglas Witten.
TED GROSS
Torah Reader Coordinator
Rabbi Gaffney Granted
Honorary Degree
(Standing left to right) Dean David Kogen, Chancellor Ismar
Schorsch, Rabbi David Gaffney and Inge Gaffney.
Rabbi Gaffney was granted the degree of Doctor of
Divinity, honories causa, by the Jewish Theological
Seminary of America.

Three Members Honored on Simchat Torah
The Festival of Sukkot reaches its joyous climax on Simhat Torah, the last day of the holiday.
Because of Israel’s perennial love of the Torah, it has been referred to in Jewish tradition as Israel’s “bride.” A tradition evolved in
ancient times to honor the individual called for the last reading of the Torah - Hatan Torah “the Groom of the Torah.” The person
honored to begin the Torah reading from its begining is called Hatan Bereshit “Groom of Genesis.” We also honor a woman who has
made a distinctive contribution to our synagogue and Jewish life.
Joseph Mizrahi
Hatan Torah
Joe Mizrahi loves the Jacksonville Jewish Cen¬
ter and the Center loves Joe Mizrahi.
For more than sixty years, Joe Mizrahi has
been actively involved in Center activities. From
his early years in the Center’s religious schools,
to being an officer of the congregation, Joe has
always been in the forefront of Center leadership.
Joe sets a good example of what a lay leader
should be. Joe has served the Center as a scout¬
master, chairman of the education committee
and youth commission. As an officer, he has
been a vice-president, secretary and treasurer.
Although asked many times, Joe would not accept
the presidency.
His love for Judaism is evident by his faithful
attendance and participation in Shabbat and holi¬
day services. It is always a delight for the congre¬
gation to hear Joe chant a Haftorah or lead a
service. His pride in his Jewish heritage is only
exceeded by his pride in his family. He has
taught his children well and they are active parti¬
cipants in religious life and leadership of the
Center. Through his example, a tradition of devo¬
tion and generosity has been firmly established.
Through his influence the Mizrahis have become
one of the leading philanthropic families in the
Jacksonville Jewish community.
A native of Jacksonville, Joe Mizrahi graduated
from the University of Florida and became a
talented CPA. Together with his brother Ralph,
Joe built a successful fashion chain from his
father’s store in downtown Jacksonville. From
his efforts, the family business has grown from
one store to many throughout the Southeast.
Over the years he shared his business expertise
with the Center finance committee and he insti¬
tuted numerous fiscal policies that enabled the
Center to be in a more responsible financial
position.
Our Hatan Torah is a loving father and grand¬
father, warm and loyal friend, faithful family mem¬
ber and exemplary Jew. His children, Eddie,
Jack and Jan and his grandchildren share in this
honor. The congregation extends to them all
their mazel tov wishes.
Robert Cantor
Hatan Bereshit
To the unknowning Center member, Robert
Cantor might seem like just another tall and
handsome fellow. But to the Center USY’ers
he’s the greatest advisor.
Ask any USY’er and you’ll be told how easy it
is to talk to Robert and how much he is admired
and respected. The feelings are definitely mutual.
Robert finds the kids refreshing and he enjoys
their honesty.
Leading a youth group requires a great déal of
organization, time, travel and patience. Robert
feels it’s worth the effort. Recently our USY won
the award for the most outstanding chapter in
the Southeast Region. This certainly can be attri¬
buted to the wonderful rapport between the
youth group and its advisor.
As if USY is not enough, Robert is chairman
of the Youth Commission, has served on the
athletic and recreation committees and was one
of the guiding forces in the formation of our
Sunday Funday program where he also served
as a volunteer coach for many years. Robert is
an active member of the Center Board.
Our Hatan Bereshit is proud of the Center’s
youth groups. He feels involved teens become
concerned and informed adults. This positive atti¬
tude during their teens can be a lasting influence
on their future Jewish lives. He kvells when he
sees some of his older USY’ers who are now
parents. “It’s rewarding to see them as adults
and just as active in Jewish life at the Center. It’s
a cycle I want to see repeated. This is my
reward” he says.
Robert’s greatest supporter is his wife Barbara.
Their sons, Billy and Steven, are active in all the
Center’s youth programs and have enjoyed their
father’s roll as youth advisor. We are grateful to
men like Robert who are there “in the beginning”
for our children.
Evelyn Mizrahi
Woman of Valor
Listening to Evelyn Mizrahi, one regrets not
being involved during her active years as a Sister¬
hood and River Garden president and dedicated
fund raiser.
In 1950, after completing a successful term as
River Garden Auxiliary president, Evelyn assumed
the chairmanship of the Center Sisterhood Beau¬
tification Committee. Under her capable leader¬
ship, the Center Sisterhood raised the necessary
funds to equip and furnish the lounge, library,
kitchen, rabbi’s study and stage of the then
newly-built Activities building on Third and Silver
Streets. No job was too great or too small for
her or her aggressive and enthusiastic commit¬
tee-whether it be cleaning chandeliers or soliciting
door to door.
Riding on the crest of this successful endeavor,
Evelyn accepted the Center Sisterhood presidency
in 1952. Her term was marked with outstanding
achievements and innovative programs.
Evelyn has also served as a den mother, Sister¬
hood vice-president and Florida Branch vice-
president. She is a Torah Fund Benefactor and a
life member of Hadassah and River Garden.
Evelyn’s love for the Center is deeply-rooted.
She credits her early inspiration to her in-laws,
Estrea and Salim Mizrahi, who encouraged her
involvement in Jewish life. Her husband, Ralph, a
past president of the Jacksonville Jewish Center
and active member in his own right, was always
supportive.
Center and community leadership are a family
affair in the Mizrahi family. Son Charles is a past
president of the Center; son David was president
of Southeast Region USY and is currently the
Center’s Religious committee chairman; daughter
Debby Katz is active in her synagogue in Fram¬
ingham. Her grandchildren, too, have held lead¬
ership roles in many Jewish youth programs.
We honor Evelyn Mizrahi for her devotion to
her family, synagogue and community. She is a
shining example of a “Woman of Valor.”

Jacksonville, Florida Chapter Hadassah
Hadassah, the Women’s Zionist Organization of America, Inc.
was born out of Henrietta Szold’s vision of a new kind of
Jewish woman and a new kind of organization to channel her
commitment and compassion for the benefit of our people.
385,000 American Jewish women are awakened to who and
what they are, and how they can live richer, fuller lives in
service to others.
Here in Jacksonville, our membership of over 1,000 has shared
a dream of major growth and achievement. Advances in re¬
search, treatment and technology have kept the Hadassah
Medical Organization at the forefront of health care world¬
wide.
The Hadassah Medical Relief Association has extended our
reach into 22 nations. We have made important gains in
nuturing both the young minds and the fragile soil of Israel.
New career programs at the Hadassah Community College, our
work with the Ethiopians through youth Aliyah, and Hadassah
Vocational Guidance Institute are all helping thousands of
young Jewish men and women to build better lives for them¬
selves and a stronger Jewish nation.
New residents and unaffiliated women are invited to join us.
Please call one of our presidents so that we may welcome you!
We wish all of you the Blessings of Health, Peace, and Joy in
the coming year.
Joan Waitz
Jacksonville Chapter of Hadassah

===== PAGE 77 =====
Sunday Funday Basketball
Exciting and Unbeatable
Gayle Bailys, Recreation Director
There were 114 children, 28 volunteer coaches and 11 volunteer referees who enjoyed a 10-game season. From Hanukkah to Purim the
gym was bustling with activity almost every Sunday. The competition was fierce but fun.
Our children learned and improved their basketball skills. Dribbling, passing, team work and of course shooting. The 8'-10' baskets
were hit, time after time. Basket after basket the points climbed. Each week with the continued support and knowledge of the coaches,
the children improved, from four baskets a game to 20. Plays were practiced and implemented, rules were learned and followed. The
excitement mounted until the very end.
It was another successful season for the Sunday Sports Assocation Basketball League. A big thank you to those who volunteered their
time and knowledge, you helped make our season a triumph.
Some were undefeatable!
SWOOSH!

United Synagogue Summer Programs
Simon Seiber presents Eric
Kravitz with the Abe Seiber
Scholarship,
Camp Ramah
Camp Ramah is a very special place where you not
only learn about Judaism, but you also live it. Anyone
who has ever visited Ramah has been touched by the
unique spirit that grows there. Ramah offers a full range
of camp athletic and social activities in addition to its
special blend of Jewish cultural and social activities.
Over the years, RAMAH has helped instill a deeper
commitment to Judaism in many of its participants. The
Center believes that benefits accrue to the congregation,
not just to the individual. We have, therefore, made
monies available to reduce the financial cost of qualified
students, regardless of need. These monies are not part
of the regular Center budget, but rather are raised by
donations to the Myra Thier Lazarus Ramah Fund,
Center Sisterhood, scholarships and from other dona¬
tions made throughout the year.
Abe Seiber established in his will a perpetual trust for
specific Jewish education purposes. With the full support
of his children, Iris and Franklin, who have never lost
interest in the Center, the proceeds of the Abe Seiber
Trust have been used to send campers to Ramah.
We are grateful to Horty Gendzier who coordinates
this worthwhile program.
RAMAH IN ISRAEL SEMINAR This six-week Jewish teen pro¬
gram for those who have completed the 11th grade is an exciting
tour of Israel. The teens study, travel and visit many kibbutzim. A
fabulous weekend is spent at the first Conservative Jewish Kibbutz—
Kibbutz Hanaton. The educational process extends far beyond the
formal classes; it is a living experience.
This year David Silverman participated in the program.
Front (L-R): Joanna Spiwak, Eric Kravitz, Kevin Kaplan, Morry
Safer, Barry Blumenthal Courtney Schuster; Back (L-R): Rebekah
Franzblau, Lee Kaplan, Jonothan Andriesse, Marcy Rubens, Emily
Goldberg, Rachel Mizrahi, Lisa Schneider. Missing from photo:
Todd Blumenthal, Aron Greenburg.
USY ON WHEELS This six and a half week cross country
tour affords teenagers, ages 14% to 18, the opportunity to
see many of the exciting sites in the U.S. and Canada while
living a fully Jewish life. Each bus of 45 teens has a chance
to visit their peers and experience Conservative Judaism in
North America.
Ramah campers received scholarships from
the Myra Thier Lazarus Fund, Sisterhood
and the Center.

Message from Rabbi David
“Judaism: An Opportunity for Active Participants”
There was a time when the change of seasons was announced by events of nature. The autumn
colors and flocking of birds, the young saplings breaking through the soil would each herald a different
season of the year. A religiously sensitive community would celebrate these events as holidays, in
festival pagentry, in community prayer and worship. Today the seasonal changes are more frequently
announced by the cries of “play ball” or “batter up.” Sports provide the means by which we note the
passing days - the football, the baseball, the basketball season - each signals the passage of time as does
the calendar. These activities are more than a form of entertainment. Sports fans have, in many cases,
found a new expression for the need to worship. Spectators at the stadium and in front of their t.v.’s;
the wholesale ^identification with our favorite sports heroes.. .our new “high priests” and charismatic
leaders, testify to the seriousness with which we consider athletic competition. Our favorite heroes and
teams are often perceived as objects of our worship.
We need to pause, prior to the advent of Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur, to realize that there
are other contests that are being waged and other activities in which the “glory of victory and the agony
of defeat” are being decided. There are contests being waged between those forces that support
Jewish identity and those that make for its dissolution; between Jewish significance and its trivilization;
between Jewish fulfillment and one or another forms of assimilation. The nature and character of
the Jewish community is being decided now...and what is sobering is that it cannot be resolved as if
Jewish life were a spectator sport. We cannot remain on the sidelines and allow the “professionals”
to play the game for us.
The renewal of Jewish life today is very much a contest and no one can say for sure what the outcome will be. The final score will be written
by those who have the will to play, or, God forbid, by those who remain inert and passive on the sidelines. The High Holidays call upon each
and everyone of us to commit to participate fully, energetically and joyously in the privilege of being a Jew in an America which gives us every
opportunity to affirm our Jewishness.
It will be fatal to our hopes for any kind of vital tomorrow to indulge in the illusion that we can do as Jews what we do as sport spectators-
pay the price of admission and let the “pros” put on the show. No one can take the place of a Jewish mother and father guiding their children
on the path of Jewish loyalty and learning. No one can take the place of parents making their home a “miniature sanctuary.” No one can take
the place of worshippers who assemble with regularity to express the highest values of their faith and to find meaning in the presence of God.
No one can take your place in supporting, strengthening and nurturing the wide range of synagogue activities that transmit the heritage of
Judaism into every aspect of our lives. The most eloquent Rabbi, the most competant educational staff do not constitute a synagogue. Only
the presence of willing hearts of men and women and children of the congregation can impart meaning, joy and strength to our collective life.
Belonging to the synagogue should mean that we take advantage of the many opportunities that our congregation makes avails us for
meaningful Jewish living. The more we participate and the more we serve the greater our achievement of making Judaism real and alive for
ourselves, our family and the community.
It is the synaogogue which provides the ideal Jewish setting for worship, where that which is noblest in ourselves reaches out towards that
which is highest in the universe - God.
It is the synagogue that best preserves the Jewish heritage and most effectively transmits the teaching of our Torah, the wisdom of our sages
and teachers.
It is the synagogue which is the most important force of Jewish continuity through the viscissitudes of our history and it is the synagogue
which continues to nourish the Jewish will to survive and provide joy in living as a Jew. It is the synagogue which raises to the loftiest significance
the great milestones, from birth to death by clothing them in the warmth of hallowed words and sacred rituals, by providing the community with
which we share these exalted occasions.
It is the synagogue which articulates and gives life to a people’s most treasured memories; which provides fellowship for those Jews who
take their heritage seriously and who look to it to provide guidance, solace and inspiration.
We find fulfillment in life by caring greatly and feeling deeply. We do so by expanding our interests and enlarging our horizons. It is realizing
that the essence of meaningful living is putting as much into life as we take out of it.
Your presence is needed at the weekly Shabbat services. If you haven’t enjoyed an early Friday evening Kabbalat Shabbat or a Saturday
afternoon Shalosh Seudot, now is the time to begin. Your presence is needed at the Daily Minyan; as a volunteer and as a participant in any
number of committees that serve our congregation.; as a student in one of our Adult Study classes. Your presence is needed as a supporter
of Sisterhood, the Family Council, the Solomon Schechter P.T.A. or Hebrew School’s A.U.T.O.; as a volunteer in our youth programs and
as a worker on behalf of the many worthy projects our synagogue sponsors. Your participation is needed in any number of worthwhile activities
that take place in the community that make possible Jewish life here and elsewhere.
When the Jewish people stood at Sinai and responded to God’s charge by saying “all that You have commanded we will do...” .they committed
themselves to leave the sidelines and to get into the game of life. Each of us is committed by that pledge at Sinai. You can make the difference
in helping to enhance the quality of Jewish life that our synagogue provides...now and for future generations. You can, at the same time, find
personal satisfaction and spiritual enrichment as you share with others in advancing the work of God. Judaism is not only our responsibility...it
can also be our fulfillment. We need fewer spectators and more participants. When the time comes when we turn in our uniforms and hang
up our glove there is no greater satisfaction than the knowledge that we have played the game...the game of life...and that we have played it
well.
Inge joins me in wishing you and yours a healthy, happy and fulfilling New Year.
Gaffney
Rabbi David and
Inna riaffnmr

===== PAGE 107 =====
Four Questions
The Jewish community is about to enter the most solemn period in the Jewish calendar, as we anticipate the arrival of the
Days of Awe, beginning with the first day of Rosh Hashanah and concluding on Yom Kippur. If we will approach these days
as holy days, not holidays, they are bound to leave a great impact on us. While we take holidays to escape duty, on holy days,
we are called to responsibility. On holidays, we seek to empty our minds, while on holy days, we attempt to enrich our spirits.
On holidays, we indulge our wants, but on holy days, we commit ourselves to what we need. On holidays, we like to change
our scenery, but on holy days, we ought to change our hearts. Rosh Hashanah is the time, therefore, for Heshbon Ha-
nefesh, for “self-evaluation”, an especially appropriate opportunity to integrate our faith, our hopes and our goals with a
higher vision of life.
In order to engage in the process of Heshbon Ha-nefesh, “self-evaluation”, the following questions need be answered.
How much am I worth?
This is the time to ask the question how much am I worth? But not in terms of money. Who was ever remembered with
affection and respect for the things of this world that he possessed? How much am i Worth in terms of the quality of my life,
my contribution to the well being of mankind, my kinship with a living God. Judaism is not opposed to affluence. Rather,
Judaism laments the absence of a social culture which spells out the obligation of money...the shared feelings, purposes,
traditions and hopes among the wealthy which will assure the appropriate utilization of a person’s material well being. This
philosophy is similar to the statement made by Tevya in “Fiddler On the Roof’’...when he said, “poverty is no crime, but it’s
no great honor either.” The genius of Judaism consists precisely in its offering a blueprint of the proper uses of wealth that
is so thoroughly lacking in our general society. That is how we can explain the astronomical sums that Jews donate to tzdaka
every year, far out of proportion to the paucity of their numbers.
The question of how much I am worth itself breaks down into three questions.
How much have I grown?
How much have I grown in this year that has passed? How has my spirit increased in stature? Eric Fromm once wrote that
“the whole life of the individual is nothing but the process of giving birth to himself. Indeed, we should be fully born when we
die - although it is a tragic fate of most individuals to die before they are born. Have I given birth to the great moral and
spiritual possibilities that lie dormant within me? Have I brought to life those potentialities for good with which God has
seeded my spirit.
How much have I ¡oved?
How much have I loved? The rare beauty and power of life is found in the blessed miracle of love. With the passing years, and
as the end of life looms not too terribly far away, many a person finds himself asking more and more deeply, have I loved
enough? Have I been able to surmount the barriers that so easily cut me off from my fellow creatures and from those people
who are closest to me and from God. People who are motivated by self-aggrandizement and who see in other people only an
audience cannot be affected by the resultant loneliness and unhappiness of their lives. Have we been able to place our
selfhood at the service of loved ones; of large social purposes, of great religious causes?
How much have I given?
How much have I given? The true substance of our lives grows within us through the process of giving. You cannot grow
without giving, for it is the giving which provides the motivation for growth. When we look within ourselves, we discover that
what we are is a sum of all that we have found ways to give to our man.
Everything of value in our lives stems from our moving outward. Perhaps, like the exploding universe it is the life force in us
exploding. We come alive when we give. Without giving, we have no real access to others, no awareness of anything except
ourselves - perhaps not even of ourselves.
These then are the great questions with which we have to search our hearts during these blessed High Holy Days, as we
remember the countless generations before us who have asked them as well. We pray this year that our responses to the
questions of life be as true, honest and those of our forebears so that we can find grace, both in the eyes of God and in our
fellow man.
A happy, healthy and fulfilling season of holy days ... to all!
East Coast Fruit Co.
Mrs. Louis Portnoy and Families

Solomon Schechter P.T.A.
The Solomon Schechter PTA is the link between faculty,
school administration and parents of the Solomon Schechter
Day School and Early Childhood Department of the Jackson¬
ville Jewish Center.
The PTA plays a significant role in supplying much needed
equipment and materials for the Day School and Pre-school
through our successful fund-raising projects.
We are able to achieve our goals through the magazine and
candy sales, two book fairs, T-shirt sale and paper drive. The
group is actively involved in the Hot Lunch Program, sponsors
the live productions by Theatreworks, Book-A-Bration, the
newsletter, “Your Child”, the Shabbat Box program and
holiday observances.
PTA also provides the parents with an opportunity to take
an active part in their child’s education.
With your help and enthusiasm we shall have a very
successful year. I look forward to working with all of you.'
Best wishes for a very happy and healthy new year.

===== PAGE 122 =====
Boy Scout Troop 14
Boy Scout Troop 14 entered its 70th year riding high on the wave of success. 1987-88
featured an active outdoor agenda coupled with programs and activities informative and
fun integrated within the framework of a modern program designed to build friendships
and character.
The Troop opened the camping season with a trip to Payne’s Prairie State Park near
Gainesville. The trip was highlighted when the boys attended a U. of Fla. football game
followed by a troop game on the astro turf of Florida Field.
St. Augustine played host to the Troop for a weekend sojourn at Anastasia State Park
along the Atlantic. The scouts’ endurance was tested by the educational, but arduous,
St. Augustine Historic Trail Hike co-sponsored by the Boy Scouts of America and The
Florida Historic Trail Society. The yeoman scouts were rewarded for their efforts with an
evening on the town at Wacky Golf in the Historic City.
Troop 14 returned to a favorite winter haunt, Blue Springs State Park. The scouts
enjoyed watching the abundant manatee that gather in the spring to escape the frigid St.
Johns River. The scouts also enjoyed swimming and snorkeling in the clear spring water
which was far more temperate than the February atmosphere. This was to be the
Troop’s only overnight weekend in cabins during the year.
We returned to Payne’s Prairie from which we embarked for a weekend of fishing aboard
a chartered boat. Although the weather discouraged the fish from biting, the scouts
experienced the unspoiled, natural beauty of Lakes Orange and Lachaloosa where
majestic bald eagles could be seen nesting along the shores. The banks of these lakes
provided the beautiful setting which inspired Margery Kinnan Rawlings to author numerous
best sellers about rustic Florida, including the “Yearling”. Ms. Rawling’s life was recently
memorialized in the film “Cross Creek”. The scouts visited the former homestead of Ms.
Rawlings in Cross Creek, and saw how Ms. Rawlings lived and wrote in that bygone era.
The weekend was capped by a visit to the unusual Wild Animal Refuge near Waldo.
A weekend campout to a new hangout, Wekiva Springs State Park, included a scenic
canoe trip and race followed by a monumental football game on the banks of the clear
natural spring.
The Troop faced an evening of voracious mosquitos at the primitive Gulf Ridge Council Scout Camp to enjoy the following day at
Busch Gardens in Tampa. Scout World saw the return of Troop 14 for a weekend of camping with 4,000 area scouts, and the boys
displayed radio controlled vehicles in an exhibit popular among scouts and visitors. Our scouts also hosted the Webloes of Cub Pack 14
overnight at nearby Camp Echockotee and assisted with the annual Cub Pack overnight.
The Blue and Gold Banquet attended by about 250 persons featured Mayor Hazouri as the guest speaker. The highlights of the
banquet was the award to Michael Weitzen of the coveted Eagle Scout award. The presentation to Michael was made by Federal Judge
Harvey Schlesinger, himself a scouting devotee. Michael has the proud distinction of being the first scout of Troop 14 to achieve the lofty
goal of Eagle in 10 years, though many have tried. Michael’s achievment was crowned by a tumultuous standing ovation by all in
attendance. Congratulations go to Michael, his parents, Dr. Jerome and Diane Weitzen, and family. (See photo)
Other programs highlighting the year included a memorable Scout Sabbath service with the Troop participating, a tour of the Florida
Publishing Company, and features on space exploration and astromony with Les Gold of NASA who is an Eagle Scout and regular guest
of Troop 14. Thanksgiving Day lunch was served to the residents of Mount Carmel by our scouts, but the real service was the magical
interaction between the young and young at heart which flows from this annual event initiated by Dan Wilensky, Asst. Scoutmaster and
Board Chairman of Mount Carmel.
Eight scouts attended summer camp at Camp Shands, they are: Michael Weitzen, Jeff Ghelerter, Marc Gentry, Life Wilensky, Andy
Ussach, Brian Usoff,Tom McCullough and Adam Dean.
Troop 14 welcomed new scouts, Morry Safer, Todd Blumenthal, Jay Goldstein, Adam Dean, Tom McCullough and Michael Garber.
Plans for the upcoming year include, among others, trips to National Scout Jamboree at Fort A. P. Hill, Virginia, NASA Cumberland
Island, U. of F. football game, and Ginni Springs.
Troop 14 is for boys entering 6th grade through age 18. Boys interested in joining may contact Scoutmaster Randy Silverberg at
398-0038 (day) or 733-6649 (evéning), Asst. Scoutmaster Harry Peltz at 737-0309, or the Center office.
Michael Weitzen receiving Eagle Award.
Brownie Troop #882
For many years, the Center Sisterhood and the Center have sponsored the Brownie
and Girl Scout troops of the Jacksonville Jewish Center. Scouting is very important
phase of the youth program. It helps develop character, usefulness at home, appreciation
of nature and the out-of-doors, creation of objects of beauty and usefulness from simple
things, and it helps these young people to find their places in the synagogue and
community through service.

Girl Scout Troop #162
Girl Scouting is more than a club, it is a nationwide, worldwide movement for girls ages 7-17. Girl Scouts make a promise and live by
the Girl Scout laws. These simple guides prepare a young girl to live in our bewildering modern world.
If you are interested in being a scout or a leader, call Clara Mann, 733-6472

===== PAGE 124 =====
Rabbi Dov Kentof and
his wife Alina
Carol Scott
Rabbi Kentof’s Secretary
Supplemental Schools
Sunday School, Afternoon School
and Hebrew High
Rabbi Dov Kentof, Director
A woman was driving a carpool of children who started
to jostle and yell. When she turned around to quiet them
she drove through a red light and almost ran down a man.
The man walked over to the car overflowing with children
and said, “Lady, don’t you know when to stop?” Glancing
back at the children, she said icily, “They aren’t all mine.”
Our lives are filled with so many distractions that we
rarely stop to think what it means to be Jewish, or just as
important, how to be Jewish. No religion or culture can
live by its thoughts and teachings alone. It needs forms and
rituals to encapsulate its truths, symbols and observances to
penetrate an individual’s inner being, and festivals and holi¬
days to celebrate its values and preserve its history. If an
ethnic or social system is to survive, it must keep its adher¬
ents constantly informed of its essential character and pur¬
pose. The ideas and ideals of a people may give it signifi¬
cance, but its group habits give it life.
As Jews, many of us have kept the holidays and customs
for years without knowing why. Others have not practiced
the basic traditions because they were never taught how.
Still others refrain from holiday observances out of embar¬
rassment born of pseudo-sophistication or because they lack
the impulse to act. We all need to know more about the
practices of Judaism. And while practice may not necessar¬
ily make perfect, at least it will help make us informed, in¬
volved, and intelligent Jews.
For the coming year, we are anticipating great involve¬
ment with our newly created Family Council, offering Jew¬
ish education to all families of our entire congregation; the
new, innovative curriculum and approach in our Afternoon
Hebrew School and High School; our new* invigorated Jun¬
ior Congregation; and our dynamic Youth Department.
I am urging the entire congregation to take advantage of
our fine Education and Youth program and to set an ex¬
ample to the younger generation by practicing Torah and
good deeds.
May this year bring you and yours fulfillment, happiness
and all the best. Alina, Gil and Tali extend their wishes to
mine.
Rabbi Dov Kentof
Director of Education & Youth

NCJW
ABOUT NCJW
Established in 1893, the National Council of Jewish Women is the oldest
Jewish women's organization in America. NCJW has more than 100,000
members in 200 Sections nationwide that are active in the organization's pri¬
ority areas of Women’s Issues, Jewish Life, Aging, Children and
Youth, Israel, and Constitutional rights.
NCJW Jacksonville Section has been instrumental in developing and
implementing many significant programs in our community, currently includ¬
ing:
P.A.C.E. - Practical and Cultural Education - a structured academic and
vocational program for teenage girls who have become involved in the court
system as status offenders.
Mental Health Forum - a one-day public forum of mini-workshops, such
as: coping with personal relationships, family life and stresses, co-sponsored
with the Mental Health Association.
Kosher Kart - delivery of balanced, hot meals to homebound individuals.
Guardian Ad Litem - a volunteer program for court appointed child advo¬
cates to represent abused and neglected children in Judicial hearings.
Tallahassee Institute - a conclave of concerned women advocating the
issues of NCJW priorities to legislative representatives.
Ship-A-Box - packaging and sending personal articles to children in Israel.
Pre-School Vision Screening - Since 1971, Council has participated in
an eye screening program for the benefit of public and private kindergarten
students. Hundreds of children have been tested under this program each
year.
Social Legislation - Through programs and study groups, and in accor¬
dance with recommendations from national and state legislative chairwomen,
this committee brings legislation to the members and recommends actions.
Green Circle Program - Volunteers present a three session dialogue with
second graders to promote a positive sense of self-worth and a better under¬
standing of human differences and similarities in children.
Art Enrichment Program - Picture Ladies - In cooperation with the
Jacksonville Art Museum and the Duval County school system, volunteers are
trained to go into classrooms on a regular basis to supplement the art resource
program in public and private schools.
Officers 1988-89
Left to right: Sandy Miller, V.P. Public Affairs; Donna Palevsky, V.P.
Community Services; Marlene Myers, Financial Secretary; Ellen Rosner,
V.P. Programs; Cheryl Blum, Treasurer; Marion Tischler, President;
Nancy Harris, V.P. Membership; Beth Shorstein, V.P. Administration;
Gertrude Kramer, Co-Corresponding Secretary. Not pictured: Adrienne
Greenhut, Co-Corresponding Secretary; Lauren Snyder, Recording
Secretary.
Women’s American ORT, Inc.
Women’s American ORT, a fundraising group which builds schools, trains teachers and provides high tech tracts of
education in various schools, is alive and active in the Jacksonville area.
ORT has schools in Israel, the U.S. and throughout 53 other countries. With our commitment to technical education,
we will see the fruition of our students by giving them the education and start they never would have had otherwise.
Locally, the Riverfront Chapter of ORT has women
from 26-85, housewives and professionals, singles and
married. We are active in community, and speak out
on Jewish issues. The group also enjoys social events.

Zionist Organization of America
Jacksonville District
To keep Israel strong, we work hard to keep the Zionist Organization of America OFFICERS
strong. Since 1948 we have generated funds for ZOA projects in Israel and Zionist D ... ,
programs in the United States. ZOA has organized young adults leadership missions, President Wendy Monlgman
instituted the first series of Christian Zionist dialogues, educated over 30,000 Jewish Chairman of the Board Milton B. Mann, MD
students at Kfar Silver Technological/Agricultural High School in Ashkelon, services Vice Presidents
over 25,000 Americans and Israelis each month with cultural programs in the ZOA . . , , . . .... . ..
House in Tel Aviv. Besides publishing The American Zionist and ZOA Insider, there Membership Lillian Meizllk
is the Zionist Information News Service that provides a weekly bulletin filled with vital Fund Raising Alan Taffet
information not usually found elsewhere. ZOA has placed ads and letters to the editor ir^^nn AnriP £hmi ihpq
on topical US-Israeli, Zionist and Jewish community issues, testified before Congress D . T ..
on Free Trade legislation and moving the US Embassy in Israel to Jerusalem and pe- Programs lna IaiI6t
titioned and demonstrated on rescinding UN resolution 3370, rejecting US dialogue Recording Secretary Leon & Lois Robins
With the PLO and opposing the sale of AWAC planes to Arab nations. It has been our Corresponding Secretary Clara Mae Mann
custom for 40 years to sponsor a Jewish National Fund Banquet, for the community, _ I 1 n 1
in order to plant trees in Israel. This year we honored the memory of Sue Balter * reasurer Joel Dressier
Becker, a past Center Sisterhood President who was very active in youth activities for Parliamentarian Nathan Shmunes
Hadassah and lived the Zionist dream by livingpart of each year in Israel with her Historian Joseph Honigman, MD
beloved husband, Jack, a leader in his own right. The children of the schools of the 1KTC ^ ai j i xa
Jacksonville Jewish Center planted a Gaiden in her memory. Guest speaker at this JINh Ulairmen Cantor A1 and Pearl Marton
banquet was the world-renown Charlotte Jacobson.
A new project is the establishing of an Ethiopian training complex at Kfar Malachi. We have been invited to place bookshelves in a library for the
Ethiopians. Last year we joined the ARMDI and B’nai B’rith to raise funds to purchase an ambulance for Israel. This will be an ongoing effort until the
goal is reached.
ZOA is one of the few Zionist organizations with a constituency whose entire agenda is the promotion of Israel. Renew your commitment to Israel
by joining with us.
Jacksonville District ZOA is proud of Michael Flaschen, this year’s winner of a ZOA/MASADA SCHOLARSHIP. Scholarships are available for students
of the Jacksonville community. The recipients may choose from the many Masada Youth Programs in Israel. These programs provide a six week tour
of Israel exploring from the Galilee to the Gulf of Eilat. The applicants must submit an essay on an assigned topic and then be interviewed by the scholarship
committee. For an application, please call Ina Taffet at 737-7848.

Stuart I. Hecht, President
Stuart I. Hecht is always glad to discuss the way B’nai
B’rith can be of service to our community and how
B’nai B’rith can provide this community with projects
and services.
Founded in 1843, B’nai B’rith is the world’s largest Jewish
organization. One-half million members in 41 countries are affiliated
with B’nai B’rith. The organization defends human rights and fights
discrimination throughout the world. It sponsors inter-faith dia¬
logues, promotes democracy and world peace and is commited to
Jewish unity. B’nai B’rith has helped instill hundreds of thousands
of young adults with moral and ethical values and a sense of Jewish
identity through the Hillel Foundations on over 400 college cam¬
puses. B'nai B’rith provides senior citizens with quality housing and
its youth organizations offer leadership training to teenagers. B’nai
B'rith works to promote Jewish education, and to preserve Jewish
culture; through community programs its membership reaches out
to those in need and works for the betterment of society.
In Jacksonville B’nai B’rith is always looking for new members
who are interested in working with the local organization. For
further information or membership information contact the
president, Stuart Hecht at 731-3406.

The Solomon Schechter Day School serves students from Kindergarten through the Seventh Grade. The school is
committed to the ideals of academic excellence, personal growth, social awareness, and Jewish values.
Schechter offers a challenging educational program with a wide range of learning opportunities in a caring atmos¬
phere. Blending American democratic heritage and Jewish ideals, we teach our children how the secular and religious
realms enrich each other and enhance our lives.
The curriculum consists of two major components, General Studies and Judaic Studies. Both areas receive profes¬
sional efforts of the highest caliber. Moreover, the two areas complement each other, so that the educational benefits of
each are enhanced by a knowledge of the other.

OFFICERS 1988-90

On the first day of creation, God said:
uYehi ohr — Let there be light! Vayehi
ohr — And there was light! and God saw
the light that it was good ”
Today we, too, need all the light of knowl¬
edge and understanding we can muster and
must make it ‘good’ for ourselves as Jews
and for all humanity. “With one heart
and one purpose” our National theme for
the coming year, we will strive as Synagogue
women to seek cultural enrichment and Jew¬
ish identification through the ethical teach¬
ings of our heritage.
Our goal is to continue to build a strong,
knowledgeable, vital Jewish community . .
right here for ourselves and for our people everywhere. We do this not
only by promoting our own educational endeavors but also by promoting
the welfare of our congregation and its schools. We sponsor educational
and cultural activities. We foster Jewish sentiments and observances in the
home. We encourage our youth with guidance and scholarships. We
support the education of Rabbis, Cantors and Teachers by participating in
the continuous building of the Jewish Theological Seminary of America.
Sisterhood can give meaning to life by encouraging its members to feel,
to think, to experience and to stand for values. Together we can realize
the many challenges which stand before us. Together we will try to help
ourselves and each other to understand and learn how to respond to make
our Sisterhood become alive with a positive accent on Jewish living, educa¬
tion and involvement.
May the coming year bring you health, happiness and peace.
Treasurer
Merle Selevan
Le Shana Tova Tikateivu.

River Garden
Medical progress has served us in many areas. One which con¬
cerns us the most is the longer lifespan of our older citizens. River
Garden Auxiliary — a very special group of dedicated ladies and
gentlemen — work diligently to help make the lives of the residents
a bit happier, a bit more comfortable, a lot more meaningful.
Our dedicated volunteers participate in. many activities that take
place at the Home. Our out-to-lunch program'gives some residents
the opportunity to go “out to eat” on a one-on-one basis. We
celebrate Mother and Father’s Day, Hanukah, and Purim through
our Special Holiday Programs. We especially recruit volunteers via
our “Give Sarah 2 Hours” drive for these programs. We sponsor a
successful blood bank drive for the use by our residents. The Sun¬
shine Program provides gifts for residents who have been hospital¬
ized as well as visits by volunteers. We staff the “Little Store”, help
with letter writing, assist with shopping and some adopt a friend.
Our fund-raising is achieved through annual dues, life member¬
ships, Tombola, Linen Shower and Donor Programs. Many friends
contribute to our Tree of Life, Memorial and Fifth Commandment
plaques.
On Anniversary Day in March, we celebrate joyfully. This day is
jointly planned and carried out by the Board of Trustees, profes¬
sional staff and hosted by the Auxiliary.
This year River Garden broke ground for its new facility in Man¬
darin. This endeavor will need the support of every Jewish family in
Jacksonville. We hope we can count on yours.
Our motto, “Service with a Heart”, is very meaningful to us.
Won’t you join our membership? We need every “Helping Hand”
and willing heart to carry out our services to the resident.
Through “Helping Hands”, our quarterly newsletter, we are able
to communicate the Auxiliary events on an annual basis.
Our best wishes to you and your families for a Healthy and
Happy New Year.
Auxiliary
Officers
President Madalyn Zager
Vice President Fund Raising Judy Price
Vice President Service Coordinator Peggy Bandel
Vice President Membership Ruth Nachman
Vice President Operations Bea Caplan
General Treasurer Dora Katz
Financial Secretary Rose Tuckman
Recording Secretary Paula Solomon
Corresponding Secretary Frances Janofsky

3 c a
The Jewish Community Alliance is a centralized facility for cultural, recreational, and
enrichment activities for the entire Jacksonville Jewish community. The dream of
many is now a reality!
The JCA provides opportunities for the development of an educated, knowledgeable
leadership and intelligent, participating citizenry. The rich and varied programs of
Jewish and general activities include:
Cultural Arts
Community Services
Social
Education
Health and Physical Education
Early Childhood
Youth
Adult and Senior Adult
Single Adult
Officers/Board of
Alexander Brest
Honorary Chairman
David Stein
President
Max Block
Vice President
Richard Browdy
Vice President
Lois Chepenik
Vice President
Lawrence DuBow
Vice President
Joseph P. Safer
Secretary
Daniel Edelman
Treasurer
Directors The JCA is a “state-of-the-art” facility, staffed by the very best professionals, whose
main goal is to serve members in a first class manner. Meeting rooms and a special
Organization Room are open for use by all local Jewish organizations.
The Jewish Community Alliance’s goal is to enhance the sense of cohesiveness
within our Jewish community, and to foster a renewed dedication to the values of
American and Jewish democratic traditions. Its mission is to serve all Jews, of any age
or circumstance.
From all of us, may your year be blessed with good health, peace, prosperity and a
feeling of K’lal Yisrael.

President’s Message
Everyone should be president of a synagogue for a day—not just any day, but on Shabbat
when there is a Bar Mitzvah. The perspective from a seat on the bimah is interesting and
thought-provoking. First, there are the usual distractions: the microphone is too loud, the air
conditioning is too low, the ark doors must be opened, etc., etc.
Attention turns to the family of the Bar Mitzvah. You can see they are nervous, excited
and so proud when their thirteen-year-old, who is now adult, does well. The parents Have
tried to make Judaism exciting and meaningful for their child by celebrating the holidays,
enrolling him in Hebrew school and having a wonderful Bar Mitzvah party. But as they place
the tallit upon his shoulders, a symbol of his responsibilities as an adult Jew, are the parents
really listening to the rabbi’s explanation of the significance of this moment? Do they realize
that this is a beginning, not an end, to the study of Torah and their child’s participation in
Jewish activities? Or, are they breathing a sigh of relief that all went well and they have
completed their responsibilities as Jewish parents?
I viewed this scenario and reflected ... I hope these parents will continue to provide for
their children a Jewish life after they become bar/bat mitzvah. I hope these parents will
make the continuation of Jewish education a priority for both their children and themselves.
I hope these children will always be comfortable in our sanctuary and that they will retain
the knowledge they have gained. I hope this special Shabbat will be a step toward a greater
understanding of their religion.
One Shabbat, come sit on the bimah with me . . .
In the New Year, may God bless us all, not with
what we want, but rather with what we need—
A sensitive heart
An open mind . . , , _,
A helping hand ÉSg ber election Marsha is
A friendly smile
An encouraging word
A caring act!
Marsha Pollock and Family
flanked by her father, Morris
Leibo and husband, Ronnie.

AUTO — Adults United to Organize
Sue Ann Blumenthal
President
Parents of students in the Jacksonville Jewish Center’s afternoon and Sunday
schools came together two years ago to establish Adults United to Organize —
AUTO. Its objective: to address problems and issues unique to children enrolled
in those schools; to build a closer liaison with the teachers; and to schedule
educational events and outings that involve the children, their families, and their
teachers. All three goals are being met.
Highlights of AUTO activities during the past year included Havdallah meet¬
ings, one of which featured a talk by Lili Meier, a survivor of the Auschwitz
concentration camp; a Shabbat weekend campout and a Sunday day trip, both
to Goldhead State Park; and a teachers appreciation day.
These activities have brought AUTO families closer together and enhanced a
sense of Jewish awareness. One visible result is increased parental attendance
and participation in Saturday morning Junior Congregation services.
Making up AUTO’s current leadership are Sue Ann Blumenthal, president;
Ellen Rubens, chairman of the board and past president; Harriet Kaplan,
faculty representative; Allan Cohen, Junior Congregation representative;
Mitchell Perin, treasurer; Cheryl LeBeau and Susan Frankel, hospitality;
Henry Blumenthal, reporter; Linda Schuster, field trip coordinator; Betty
Edwards, corresponding secretary; Debra Penna and Arlene Rademan,
membership; Jan Klein and George Rubens, historians; Rabbi Dov Ken-
tof, spiritual leader; and phone coordinator, George Rubens.
AUTO families enjoyed picnics and campouts . . .

RIVER
GARDEN
Hebrew Home for the Aged
n
River Garden Hebrew Home River Garden Holding Company
Jack Price President
Lee Horovitz Chairperson,
Admissions Board
Martin Wolf Treasurer

Martin Goetz, MS, MPA Administrator
Herman Paul.. ££***. President
Arthur Gutman Vice President
Peter Winterfiekl Secretary
Elliott Palevsky, MA Executive Director
The River Garden Hebrew Home . . .
Keeper of the “Fifth Commandment*'
“Honor thy father and mother that thy days may be long ...”
This is the Fifth Commandment and the only one which has within it a very special reward. The
theme of respect for our forebears and intergenerational responsibility is central to the
organization of Jewish life.
It was this commandment which inspired the founding of River Garden Hebrew Home in 1946.
It was this commandment which nourished the love and care that over the years has made River
Garden a nationally recognized model of excellence in its field.
It is this commandment which underlies the tradition of meeting 100% of local Jewish
community needs and of serving rich and poor alike with excellence and caring. To be served all
you need do is call!
River Garden’s well established programs of short-term rehabilitative care and long-term
residential Care serve over 180 people on any given day. These programs are guided by a devoted,
professional full time staff of over 200. This staff includes physicians and beauticians, nurses and
artists, social workers and bakers, pharmacist and activities specialists, physical, occupational and
speech therapists, dieticians and musicians. In addition, scores of dedicated volunteers contribute
of themselves to help create a special caring community at Stockton Street and Riverside Avenue
on the St. Johns River.
When River Garden occupies its new campus towards the end of 1989 these programs will be
augmented by Out-Patient and Family Care Support Services helping to meet the needs of those
living in the larger community. There will also be a host of new residential choices on the campus
itself.
The new River Garden campus is on 32 rural acres in Mandarin, near the intersection of 1-295
and Old St. Augustine Road. The design of the centers for Long-Term Care and Short-Term
Rehabilitation recognizes that people with medical needs do not have to live in medical settings.
There will be religious, social and recreational areas: a chapel, a theatre; hobby shops and beauty
shops; a variety of dining and eating places; water walks and wooded walks; a medical services/
wellness center, and recreation courts. The new River Garden Home will be part of a larger
community which will include apartments and villas; a town center; a general store; a fitness center,
and a campus club.
Overall, River Garden is today and tomorrow a community where people live, love, and
care; a community of many life styles and of rich Jewish content, and a community which
every day strives to live up to the mandate of the Fifth Commandment.

Jewish Women’s Bowling League
One of the best kept secrets in Jacksonville may be the Jewish Women’s Bowling League that meets on Tuesday
mornings, 9:15, at the Mandarin Bowling Alley.
Officers for 1988-89 are: Wendy Honigman, President; Elaine Mack, Vice-President; Phyllis Raderman, Secretary; Shirley
Schinasi, Treasurer.
At the closing “awards” luncheon, the women enjoyed this poem by Michelle Frisch, with some assistance from her
mother, Eleanor Dorman, that really explains what the league is all about.

The Sunday School
A ”Hands on” Experience
The children of our Sunday School program learn
about holidays, Bible stories, life cycle events, and
symbols of Judaism. Through song, dance music,
arts and crafts, storytelling, games and a variety of
other activities, children learn about these topics by
experiencing them in creative and innovative ways.
Children also become familiar with the synagogue as
a comfortable, secure, and caring place. In addition,
the values of tzedakah and helping others are
emphasized.
Teachers: Alina Kentof - Coordinator, Susie Kadish and Nava Hod
Students:
Kindergarten - Adele Blum, Mitchell Eaglstein, Cale Margol, Jeffrey Pies, Scott Sandler, Jodie Snyder, David Trager,
Blake Eber, Amy Helming, Meredith Picray.

Lyle Passink, Meredith Perin, Alyssa Rademan, Joshua Edwards.

===== PAGE 188 =====
Cub Scout Pack 14
Cub Scout Pack 14 had an outstanding year. Under the sponsorship of the Jewish Center, this group of 7 through 10-year-old boys has
grown to number 43 active and motivated scouts. Smaller den meetings were held once a week either at the Center or in the den leader’s
home. Pack meetings with all the boys and their families were held once a month at the Center.
Cub Scouts aim high: at developing good character, spiritual growth, good citizenship, sportsmanship, comaraderie with other boys,
and a sense of personal achievement. Thanks to our extremely competent and caring den leaders (Sue Ann Blumenthal, completing her
fourth year, Laurie Farrington, Linda Schuster, Brenda Brown, and Gary Levine) the Cubs have earned many badges of achievement
while having fun. Trips to the museum, Marineland, UNF nature trails, television stations are but a few examples of the dens’ high interest
activities.
Led by Cubmaster Lanny Zimmerman, out pack meetings have been highly successful. The excitement of Pinewood Derby, speakers
from the Audobon Society, Fire and Rescue, and many more added to the delightful presentations of the dens. Scout Sabbath, our own
Friday evening service, was led by a parent, Sandy Kadish, and all of the scouts attending. A great time was had by all attending our
father-son campout preceeded by a family dinner and swim on the Center grounds. The highlight of our year was the Blue and Gold
Banquet with our special guest speaker, the Honorable Mayor Tommy Hazouri. How proud the boys and families were as he personally
presented them their awards and answered their questions.
Along with the den leaders, our success depended on the Pack Committee. Special thanks to Lanny Zimmerman, Anne DePaul, Steve
Albert, and Dana Garfinkel. Cub Scouts is based on adult support and involvement. Please call the Center office if you can help:
Happy Scouting,
Carole Goldberg
Pack Committee Chairman

This Yearbook is Dedicated to the Memory of
Esther G. Fishier
The Jacksonville Jewish Center takes this occasion to dedicate this yearbook in memory of
ESTHER FISHLER, may her memory be for a blessing. For more than thirty-three years, Esther
was the guiding force for so many of the Center's fund-raising programs. She started in
Education and then was asked by Rabbi Tofield to become involved in the yearbook advertising.
Each yearbook was better than the last, because she set higher goals for every subsequent
yearbook. It was literally a 24-hour a day job for Esther. Even on her way home from work she
would stop by a potential advertiser. During her years as advertising editor, the yearbook
revenues increased 21/2 times. But to Esther it was not a job, but a labour of love.
It seems the telephone was invented for Esther Fishier," wrote Bob Gast, former
executive director in 1977," for in her 26 years at the Jewish Center she has probably set a world's
record for calling people." She called for volunteers for the minyan, sponsors for holiday flowers
and receptions, invitations, cash donations, raffle tickets, membership enrollment and, of
course, yearbook advertising. To each task she brought enthusiasm and unselfish dedication.
When Esther started something, she completed it.
She was a devoted volunteer of Center Sisterhood. As Membership Vice-President, she
had one of the largest new member enrollments. For years she co-chaired the card party. As
Donor co-chair she raised $7,000, the largest amount ever. This was what she truly enjoyed—
raising money for the Center.
"Anyone that knew Esther knew her love of life. She was a person who lived fully and
intensely...she was the epitome of energy," eulogized Rabbi Gaffney. She put that energy to good
use...for the betterment of her family, her synagogue, her friends. She met life's challenges with
dignity and optimism.
The Center Yearbook was like her baby. She nurtured it with her love, she gave it life with
her energy. Every year when we receive a yearbook, we will each share Esther’s legacy to the
congregation.
Isabel Balotin
Editor and Dear Friend

JACKSONVILLE JEWISH CENTER MASTER CALENDAR 1988-89

On the surface Mazel Mania looks like just a fabulous social event, but it
is really much more. This casino game night is one of the Center’s best
volunteer efforts because it is a dramatic display of the active involvement
of more than 140 Center members and newcomers.
We thank everyone for contributing to the success of the evening and
extend special recognition to the coordinators, Carla Bernard and Isabel
Balotin, ably assisted by Ava Axelrod, Etta Drashin, Marilyn Garber, Zelda
Greenberg, George Levin, Pam Picray, Rena Schoenberg, Ina Taffet and
Kathie Wallach.

Cheryl Blum, Fred Blum, Marlene Bossen, Ruchel Broadman, Stephen Broadman, Eloise Bono, Sandy Burman, Sherwin Burman,

Kornblum, Linda Kunsberg, Ralph Kunsberg, Pearl Levin, Phyllis Levin, Lillie Ray Levy, BeBe Marin, Alan Marks, Jo Marks, Debbie

Center Awarded for SIMCHA 25
Last spring, the Jacksonville Jewish Center submitted an excellent
compilation of the very successful SIMCHA 25 program which was
held in January 1986. To our delight, SIMCHA 25 won the Solomon
Schechter Unique Program Award for its high standards of organiza¬
tion, implementation, effectiveness and creativity.
At the United Synagogue Convention in November, President
Gene Kornblum and Horty Gendzier proudly accepted the prestigious
award on behalf of the congregation. To be recognized by a national
organization as widely respected as United Synagogue was a thrilling
experience for Gene and Horty as it was for the Center delegates,
Rabbi and Mrs. Gaffney, Annetta Kornblum, Sheldon Gendzier and
Wendy and Joe Honigman.
SIMCHA 25 — OUR SEASON OF TORAH embraced three im¬
portant and closely related occasions of our congregation: The Silver
Anniversary of the Solomon Schechter Day School; the dedication of
a Sefer Torah; and the establishment of the Jacksonville Jewish Cen¬
ter Educational Trust Fund. Coordinated by Sheila Jacobson, this
event, so far, has been the most far reaching and enduring celebration
in the history of our synagogue.
We are proud to be recognized for our achievements, but we are
far more grateful for the continuous generosity of our congregation.
Once again, our appreciation to Sheila Jacobson, all the volunteer
workers and Center staff who made this event so successful. Our
thanks to Peggy Organes and Isabel Balotin for compiling the beauti¬
ful SIMCHA 25 award presentation.
